<li><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Wales_(1959%E2%80%93present).svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of Wales (1959–present).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/16px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" width="16" height="10" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/24px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="498" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Wales" title="Portal:Wales">Wales  &#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template talk:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> is a country in Western Europe that has a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> including its own <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">language</a>, customs, holidays and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Wales" title="Music of Wales">music</a>. Wales is primarily represented by the symbol of the red <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Dragon" title="Welsh Dragon">Welsh Dragon</a>, but other <a href="/wiki/National_emblem" title="National emblem">national emblems</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daffodil" class="mw-redirect" title="Daffodil">daffodil</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> words for leeks (<i>cennin</i>) and daffodils (<i>cennin Pedr</i>, lit. "(Saint) Peter's Leeks") are closely related and it is likely that one of the symbols came to be used due to a misunderstanding for the other one, though it is less clear which came first.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_Welsh_culture">Development of Welsh culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development of Welsh culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_influences">Historical influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Wales" title="History of Wales">History of Wales</a></div>
<p>Although Wales has been identified as having been inhabited by humans for some 230,000 years, as evidenced by the discovery of a <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Bontnewydd_Palaeolithic_site" title="Bontnewydd Palaeolithic site">Bontnewydd Palaeolithic site</a> in North Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies19933_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies19933-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> it is the Welsh rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> who have proven to be the most influential.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Building on the construction<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in Wales during the <a href="/wiki/Wales_in_the_Roman_era" title="Wales in the Roman era">Roman era</a> of occupation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesMattingly1990153_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesMattingly1990153-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> these<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2016)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> early kingdoms were also influenced by Ireland; but precise details are unclear prior to the 8th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies198294_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies198294-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Wales" title="List of rulers of Wales">Kingdoms</a> arose at that time, including <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwynedd" title="Kingdom of Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deheubarth" title="Deheubarth">Deheubarth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1982102_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1982102-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_VII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/King_Henry_VII.jpg/170px-King_Henry_VII.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/King_Henry_VII.jpg/255px-King_Henry_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/King_Henry_VII.jpg/340px-King_Henry_VII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1865" data-file-height="2757" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_VII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry Tudor, descended from the Tudors of Penmynydd, became King Henry VII of England</div></div></div>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Rhodri_the_Great" title="Rhodri the Great">Rhodri the Great</a> in the 9th century was the first ruler to oversee a large portion of Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaund200650-54_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaund200650-54-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> it was not until 1055 that <a href="/wiki/Gruffydd_ap_Llywelyn" title="Gruffydd ap Llywelyn">Gruffydd ap Llywelyn</a> united the individual Welsh kingdoms and began to annex parts of England. Gruffydd was killed by his own men on 5 August 1063 while <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold Godwinson</a> sought to engage him in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaund200687-97_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaund200687-97-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This was just over three years before the <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman invasion of England">Norman invasion of England</a>, which led to a drastic change of fortune for Wales. By 1070, the Normans had already seen successes in their <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Wales" title="Norman invasion of Wales">invasion of Wales</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwent" title="Kingdom of Gwent">Gwent</a> fallen and Deheubarth plundered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies198728-30_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies198728-30-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The invasion was seemingly complete by 1093.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1911398_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1911398-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the Welsh rebelled against their new overlords the following year, and the Welsh kingdoms were re-established and most of the land retaken from the Normans over the subsequent decades.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaund2006162-171_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaund2006162-171-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> While Gwynedd grew in strength, Powys was broken up after the death of <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Madog" title="Llywelyn ap Madog">Llywelyn ap Madog</a> in the 1160s and was never reunited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1911508-509_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1911508-509-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_the_Great" title="Llywelyn the Great">Llywelyn the Great</a> rose in Gwynedd and had reunited the majority of Wales by his death in 1240.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2005124_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2005124-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> After his death, King <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a> intervened to prevent <a href="/wiki/Dafydd_ap_Llywelyn" title="Dafydd ap Llywelyn">Dafydd ap Llywelyn</a> from inheriting his father's lands outside Gwynedd, leading to war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1911693_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1911693-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The claims of his successor, <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a>, conflicted with those of King <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a>; this resulted in <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">the conquest</a> of Wales by English forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarpenter2003510_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarpenter2003510-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tudors_of_Penmynydd" title="Tudors of Penmynydd">Tudors of Penmynydd</a> grew in power and influence during the 13th to 15th centuries, first owning land in North Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffithsThomas198517_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffithsThomas198517-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> but losing it after <a href="/wiki/Maredudd_ap_Tudur" title="Maredudd ap Tudur">Maredudd ap Tudur</a> backed the 1400 uprising of <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glyndŵr">Owain Glyndŵr</a>. Maredudd's son, Owain ap Maredudd ap Tudur, anglicised his name to become <a href="/wiki/Owen_Tudor" title="Owen Tudor">Owen Tudor</a>, and was the grandfather of Henry Tudor.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Henry took the throne of England following the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> when his forces defeated those of <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaynesmith200581_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaynesmith200581-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes197249_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes197249-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">House of Tudor</a> continued to reign through several successive monarchs until 1603, when <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James I</a> (James VI of Scotland) took the throne for the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>; his great grandmother was <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity_and_nationalism">Identity and nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Identity and nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh nationalism</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_symbols_of_Wales" title="National symbols of Wales">National symbols of Wales</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:433px;max-width:433px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg/225px-Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg" width="225" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg/338px-Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg/450px-Daffodils_St_David%27s_Day_at_the_Senedd_2012_Dydd_G%C5%B5yl_Dewi_yn_y_Senedd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Leeks_bg.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Leeks_bg.JPG/200px-Leeks_bg.JPG" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Leeks_bg.JPG/300px-Leeks_bg.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Leeks_bg.JPG/400px-Leeks_bg.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1224" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Daffodils and leeks, two of the national symbols of Wales</div></div></div>
<p>Official symbols of Wales include the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Dragon" title="Welsh Dragon">Welsh Dragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(plant)" title="Narcissus (plant)">daffodil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a>. Both the dragon and leek date back to the 7th century, as King <a href="/wiki/Cadwaladr" title="Cadwaladr">Cadwaladr</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwynedd" title="Kingdom of Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> had his soldiers wear the vegetable during battle against <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> to make it easier to identify them.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcleek_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcleek-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He also introduced the Red Dragon standard,<sup id="cite_ref-dragonwar_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonwar-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> although this symbol was most likely introduced to the British Isles by Roman troops.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It may also have been a reference to the 6th century Welsh word <i>draig</i>, which meant "leader".<sup id="cite_ref-dragonimport_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonimport-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The standard was appropriated by the Normans during the 11th century, and used for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Standard_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Standard of Scotland">Royal Standard of Scotland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I of England</a> took a red dragon standard with him on the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dragonwar_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonwar-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The colours of the leek were used for the uniforms of soldiers under <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcleek_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcleek-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both symbols were popular with <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">Tudor</a> kings, with <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a> (Henry Tudor) adding the white and green background to the red dragon standard.<sup id="cite_ref-dragonwar_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonwar-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It was largely forgotten by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>, who favoured a <a href="/wiki/Unicorn" title="Unicorn">unicorn</a> instead.<sup id="cite_ref-dragonimport_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonimport-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> By the 17th and 18th centuries, it became common practice in Great Britain for the gentry to wear leeks on <a href="/wiki/St._David%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="St. David&#39;s Day">St. David's Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcleek_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcleek-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 1807, a "a red dragon passant standing on a mound" was made the King's badge for Wales. Following an increase in nationalism in 1953, it was proposed to add the motto <i>Y ddraig goch ddyry cychwyn</i> ("the red dragon takes the lead") to the flag. This was poorly received, and six years later Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> intervened to put the current flag in place.<sup id="cite_ref-dragonimport_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragonimport-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The daffodil is a more recent development, becoming popular during the 19th century. It may have been linked to the leek; as the Welsh for daffodil (<i>cenhinen Bedr</i>) translates as "St Peter's Leek". During the 20th century, the daffodil rose to rival the prominence of the leek as a symbol of Wales. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> ensured that the daffodil had a place in the investiture of <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Edward, Prince of Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcleek_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcleek-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Welsh_costume" title="Traditional Welsh costume">traditional Welsh costume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_hat" title="Welsh hat">Welsh hat</a> were well known during the 19th and early 20th centuries. <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Princess Alexandrina Victoria</a> (later Queen Victoria) had a hat made for her when she visited Wales in 1832. The hat was popularised by <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Curnow_Vosper" title="Sydney Curnow Vosper">Sydney Curnow Vosper</a>'s 1908 painting <i><a href="/wiki/Salem_(Vosper_painting)" title="Salem (Vosper painting)">Salem</a></i>, but by then its use had declined.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Wales" title="Languages of Wales">Languages of Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_English" title="Welsh English">Welsh English</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Wales" title="Religion in Wales">Religion in Wales</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Welsh_saints" title="List of Welsh saints">List of Welsh saints</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Saint_David.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_Saint_David.svg/220px-Flag_of_Saint_David.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_Saint_David.svg/330px-Flag_of_Saint_David.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_Saint_David.svg/440px-Flag_of_Saint_David.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1620" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Saint_David.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Saint_David" title="Flag of Saint David">Flag of Saint David</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> occupation, the dominant religion in Wales was a <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> one, led by the <a href="/wiki/Druids" class="mw-redirect" title="Druids">druids</a>. Little is known about the traditions and ceremonies, but <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, whose claims were sometimes exaggerated, stated that they performed <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifice</a>: he says that in AD 61, an altar on <a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a> was found to be "drenched with the blood of their prisoners".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was introduced to Wales through the Romans, and after they abandoned the British Isles, it survived in South East Wales at <a href="/wiki/Hentland" title="Hentland">Hentland</a>. In the 6th century, this was home to <a href="/wiki/Dubricius" title="Dubricius">Dubricius</a>, the first Celtic saint.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest religion in modern Wales is Christianity, with almost 58% of the population describing themselves as <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> in the 2011 census.<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_of_Wales" title="Presbyterian Church of Wales">Presbyterian Church of Wales</a> was for many years the largest denomination; it was born out of the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Methodist_revival" title="Welsh Methodist revival">Welsh Methodist revival</a> in the 18th century and seceded from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> in 1811;<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> had an average Sunday attendance of 32,171 in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-nolonger_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nolonger-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>, and was also part of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, but was disestablished by the British Government in 1920 under the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Church_Act_1914" title="Welsh Church Act 1914">Welsh Church Act 1914</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Non-Christian religions have relatively few followers in Wales, with <a href="/wiki/Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> making up 1.5% of the population while <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a> represent 0.3% each in the 2011 census. Over 32% of the population in Wales did not note a religion.<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Research in 2007 by the <a href="/wiki/Tearfund" title="Tearfund">Tearfund</a> organisation showed that Wales had the lowest average church attendance in the UK, with 12% of the population routinely attending.<sup id="cite_ref-nolonger_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nolonger-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Holidays" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Holidays">Welsh Holidays</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of Wales is <a href="/wiki/Saint_David" title="Saint David">Saint David</a>, <i>Dewi Sant</i> in Welsh. <a href="/wiki/St._David%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="St. David&#39;s Day">St. David's Day</a> is celebrated on 1 March,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> which some people argue should be designated a public holiday in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Other days which have been proposed for national public commemorations are 16 September (the day on which <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glyndŵr">Owain Glyndŵr</a>'s rebellion began)<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and 11 December (the death of <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a>).
</p><p>The traditional seasonal festivals in Wales are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Calan_Gaeaf" title="Calan Gaeaf">Calan Gaeaf</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Hallowe%27en" class="mw-redirect" title="Hallowe&#39;en">Hallowe'en</a> or <a href="/wiki/Samhain" title="Samhain">Samhain</a>-type festival on the first day of winter)<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnon18392-4_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnon18392-4-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gwyl_Fair_y_Canhwyllau" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwyl Fair y Canhwyllau">Gwyl Fair y Canhwyllau</a> (literally Mary's Festival of the Candles, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Candlemas" title="Candlemas">Candlemas</a>; also coinciding with <a href="/wiki/Imbolc" title="Imbolc">Imbolc</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calan_Mai" title="Calan Mai">Calan Mai</a> (<a href="/wiki/May_Day" title="May Day">May Day</a>, and similar to <a href="/wiki/Beltane" title="Beltane">Beltane</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calan_Awst" class="mw-redirect" title="Calan Awst">Calan Awst</a> (1 August, equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Lammas" title="Lammas">Lammas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lughnasa" class="mw-redirect" title="Lughnasa">Lughnasa</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/G%C5%B5yl_Mabsant" title="Gŵyl Mabsant">Gŵyl Mabsant</a> celebrated by each parish in commemoration of its native saint, often marked by a fair<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dydd_Santes_Dwynwen" title="Dydd Santes Dwynwen">Dydd Santes Dwynwen</a>, a Welsh equivalent to St Valentine's Day<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calennig" title="Calennig">Calennig</a> is a Welsh New Year celebration<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_art" title="Welsh art">Welsh art</a></div>
<p>Many works of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Celtic art</a> have been found in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Medieval">Early Medieval</a> period, the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> of Wales participated in the <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a> of the British Isles and a number of <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> <a href="/wiki/Category:Illuminated_manuscripts_of_Welsh_origin" title="Category:Illuminated manuscripts of Welsh origin">possibly of Welsh origin</a> survive, of which the 8th century <a href="/wiki/Hereford_Gospels" title="Hereford Gospels">Hereford Gospels</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lichfield_Gospels" title="Lichfield Gospels">Lichfield Gospels</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> are the most notable. The 11th century <a href="/wiki/Ricemarch_Psalter" title="Ricemarch Psalter">Ricemarch Psalter</a> (now in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>) is certainly Welsh, made in <a href="/wiki/St_David%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="St David&#39;s">St David's</a>, and shows a late Insular style<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> with unusual Viking influence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawlor1914ix-xii_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawlor1914ix-xii-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The best of the few Welsh artists of the 16th-18th centuries tended to move elsewhere to work, but in the 18th century the dominance of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Landscape art">landscape art</a> in <a href="/wiki/English_art" title="English art">English art</a> brought them motives to stay at home, and brought an influx of artists from outside to paint Welsh scenery. The Welsh painter <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wilson_(painter)" title="Richard Wilson (painter)">Richard Wilson</a> (1714–1782) is arguably the first major British landscapist, but rather more notable for Italian scenes than Welsh ones, although he did paint several on visits from London.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Bard_(1774).jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg/220px-The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg/330px-The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg/440px-The_Bard_%281774%29.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="665" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Bard_(1774).jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Bard</i>, 1774, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jones_(artist)" title="Thomas Jones (artist)">Thomas Jones</a> (1742–1803)</div></div></div>
<p>It remained difficult for artists relying on the Welsh market to support themselves until well into the 20th century. An <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> in 1854 provided for the establishment of a number of art schools throughout the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wales_Institute,_Cardiff" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Wales Institute, Cardiff">Cardiff School of Art</a> opened in 1865.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Graduates still very often had to leave Wales to work, but <a href="/wiki/Betws-y-Coed" title="Betws-y-Coed">Betws-y-Coed</a> became a popular centre for artists, and its artists' colony helped form the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Cambrian_Academy_of_Art" title="Royal Cambrian Academy of Art">Royal Cambrian Academy of Art</a> in 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The sculptor Sir William <a href="/wiki/Goscombe_John" title="Goscombe John">Goscombe John</a> made many works for Welsh commissions, although he had settled in London.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Williams_(Welsh_artist)" title="Christopher Williams (Welsh artist)">Christopher Williams</a>, whose subjects were mostly resolutely Welsh, was also based in London.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Stephens" title="Thomas E. Stephens">Thomas E. Stephens</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Vicari" title="Andrew Vicari">Andrew Vicari</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> had very successful careers as portraitists based respectively in the United States and France. Sir <a href="/wiki/Frank_Brangwyn" title="Frank Brangwyn">Frank Brangwyn</a> was Welsh by origin, but spent little time in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Perhaps the most famous Welsh painters, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_John" title="Augustus John">Augustus John</a> and his sister <a href="/wiki/Gwen_John" title="Gwen John">Gwen John</a>, mostly lived in London and Paris;<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> however the landscapists Sir <a href="/wiki/Kyffin_Williams" title="Kyffin Williams">Kyffin Williams</a><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_obit_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_obit-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Prendergast_(artist)" title="Peter Prendergast (artist)">Peter Prendergast</a><sup id="cite_ref-Tribute_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribute-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> remained living in Wales for most of their lives, though well in touch with the wider art world. <a href="/wiki/Ceri_Richards" title="Ceri Richards">Ceri Richards</a> was very engaged in the Welsh art scene as a teacher in Cardiff, and even after moving to London; he was a figurative painter in international styles including <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">Surrealism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tate_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tate-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Various artists have moved to Wales, including <a href="/wiki/Eric_Gill" title="Eric Gill">Eric Gill</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> the London-born Welshman <a href="/wiki/David_Jones_(poet)" class="mw-redirect" title="David Jones (poet)">David Jones</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and the sculptor <a href="/wiki/Jonah_Jones_(sculptor)" title="Jonah Jones (sculptor)">Jonah Jones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Kardomah_Gang" title="The Kardomah Gang">The Kardomah Gang</a> was an intellectual circle centred on the poet <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas" title="Dylan Thomas">Dylan Thomas</a> and poet and artist <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Watkins" title="Vernon Watkins">Vernon Watkins</a> in Swansea, which also included the painter <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Janes" title="Alfred Janes">Alfred Janes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gang_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gang-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Today much art is produced in Wales, as elsewhere in a great diversity of styles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceramics">Ceramics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ceramics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historically, there were three main areas of pottery production in Wales: south-west Wales, northern Monmouthshire and the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Glamorgan" title="Vale of Glamorgan">Vale of Glamorgan</a>. Several further sites can be identified through their place names, for example <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pwllcrochan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pwllcrochan (page does not exist)">Pwllcrochan</a> in Pembrokshire, which translates to Crock Pool, and archaeology has also revealed former <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> sites across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBebb19974_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebb19974-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> These were often located near <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> beds, for ease of resource gathering.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBebb19975_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebb19975-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buckley" title="Buckley">Buckley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ewenny_Pottery" title="Ewenny Pottery">Ewenny</a> became leading areas of pottery production in Wales during the 17th and 18th centuries; these are applied as generic terms to different potters within those areas during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBebb19976_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebb19976-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> South Wales had several notable <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">potteries</a> during that same period, an early exponent being the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Pottery" title="Cambrian Pottery">Cambrian Pottery</a> (1764–1870, also known as "<a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a> pottery"). The works from Cambrian attempted to imitate those of <a href="/wiki/Wedgwood" title="Wedgwood">Wedgwood</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nantgarw_Pottery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nantgarw Pottery">Nantgarw Pottery</a>, near Cardiff, was in operation from 1813 to 1823 making fine <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>. <a href="/wiki/Llanelli" title="Llanelli">Llanelly Pottery</a> was the last surviving major pottery works in South Wales when it closed in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Welsh-language_literature" title="Welsh-language literature">Welsh-language literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_literature_in_English" title="Welsh literature in English">Welsh literature in English</a></div><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_mythology" title="Welsh mythology">Welsh mythology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatre">Theatre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Theatre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Wales" title="Theatre of Wales">Theatre of Wales</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:2508px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg" class="image" title="360° panoramic view of the amphitheatre at Caerleon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg/2500px-360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg" width="2500" height="251" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg/3750px-360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg/5000px-360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="23552" data-file-height="2368" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:360%C2%B0_panorama_of_amphitheater_at_Caerleon.jpg" title="File:360° panorama of amphitheater at Caerleon.jpg"> </a></div>360° panoramic view of the amphitheatre at Caerleon</div>
</div></div>
<p>Theatrical performances are thought to have begun after the Roman invasion of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-theatreroycom_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatreroycom-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> There are remains of a <a href="/wiki/Roman_amphitheatre" title="Roman amphitheatre">Roman amphitheatre</a> at <a href="/wiki/Caerleon" title="Caerleon">Caerleon</a>, which would have served the nearby fortress of <a href="/wiki/Isca_Augusta" title="Isca Augusta">Isca Augusta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Between Roman and modern times, theatre in Wales was limited to performances of travelling players, sometimes in temporary structures. Welsh theatrical groups also performed in England, as did English groups in Wales. The rise of the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> in the 17th century and then Methodism during the 18th century caused declines in Welsh theatre as performances were seen as immoral.<sup id="cite_ref-theatreroycom_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatreroycom-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite this, performances continued on showgrounds, and with a handful of travelling groups of actors.<sup id="cite_ref-theatreroycom_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatreroycom-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre,_Monmouth" title="Savoy Theatre, Monmouth">Savoy Theatre, Monmouth</a>, the oldest theatre still in operation in Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> was built during the 19th century and originally operated as the Assembly Rooms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKissack2003142-144_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKissack2003142-144-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Other theatres opened over the following decades, with Cardiff's Theatre Royal opening in 1827. After a fire, a replacement <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales_Theatre,_Cardiff" title="Prince of Wales Theatre, Cardiff">Theatre Royal</a> opened in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WMailOct1878_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMailOct1878-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Competition for theatres led to further buildings being constructed, such as the <a href="/wiki/New_Theatre,_Cardiff" title="New Theatre, Cardiff">New Theatre, Cardiff</a>, which opened on 10 December 1906.<sup id="cite_ref-New2_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New2-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Television_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Television in the United Kingdom">Television in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Welsh-language_television_channels" title="List of Welsh-language television channels">List of Welsh-language television channels</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Wales" title="Cinema of Wales">Cinema of Wales</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Wales" title="Music of Wales">Music of Wales</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG/220px-CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG/330px-CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG/440px-CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CardiffArmsParkMaleChoir.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cardiff Arms Park male voice choir</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crwth_rem.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Crwth_rem.jpg/220px-Crwth_rem.jpg" width="220" height="487" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Crwth_rem.jpg/330px-Crwth_rem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Crwth_rem.jpg/440px-Crwth_rem.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1359" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crwth_rem.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval <a href="/wiki/Crwth" title="Crwth">crwth</a> instrument</div></div></div>
<p>Wales is often referred to as "the land of song",<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and is notable for its harpists, male choirs, and solo artists. The principal Welsh festival of music and poetry is the annual <i><a href="/wiki/National_Eisteddfod" class="mw-redirect" title="National Eisteddfod">National Eisteddfod</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Llangollen <a href="/wiki/International_Eisteddfod" class="mw-redirect" title="International Eisteddfod">International Eisteddfod</a></i> echoes the National Eisteddfod but provides an opportunity for the singers and musicians of the world to perform.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Traditional music and dance in Wales is supported by many societies. The Welsh Folk Song Society has published a number of collections of songs and tunes.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Male choirs (sometimes called male voice choirs), which emerged in the 19th century, have remained a lasting tradition in Wales. Originally these choirs were formed as the tenor and bass sections of chapel choirs, and embraced the popular secular hymns<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008532_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008532-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Many of the historic Welsh choirs survive, singing a mixture of traditional and popular songs.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Traditional instruments of Wales include <i>telyn deires</i> (<a href="/wiki/Triple_harp" title="Triple harp">triple harp</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008179_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008179-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> fiddle,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008281_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008281-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crwth" title="Crwth">crwth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008353_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008353-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <i>pibgorn</i> (hornpipe) and other instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008677_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008677-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cerdd_Dant" title="Cerdd Dant">Cerdd Dant</a> Society promotes its specific singing art primarily through an annual one-day festival.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BBC_National_Orchestra_of_Wales" title="BBC National Orchestra of Wales">BBC National Orchestra of Wales</a> performs in Wales and internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_National_Opera" title="Welsh National Opera">Welsh National Opera</a> is based at the <a href="/wiki/Wales_Millennium_Centre" title="Wales Millennium Centre">Wales Millennium Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Bay" title="Cardiff Bay">Cardiff Bay</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/National_Youth_Orchestra_of_Wales" title="National Youth Orchestra of Wales">National Youth Orchestra of Wales</a> was the first of its type in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG/220px-Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG/330px-Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG/440px-Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1660" data-file-height="1091" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Janis_Joplin_Tom_Jones_1969.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tom Jones performing with <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a> in 1969</div></div></div>
<p>Wales has had a number of successful singers across the decades. In the 1960s, bands such as <a href="/wiki/Amen_Corner_(band)" title="Amen Corner (band)">Amen Corner</a>, The Iveys/<a href="/wiki/Badfinger" title="Badfinger">Badfinger</a> and singers including <a href="/wiki/Tom_Jones_(singer)" title="Tom Jones (singer)">Sir Tom Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Bassey" title="Shirley Bassey">Dame Shirley Bassey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Hopkin" title="Mary Hopkin">Mary Hopkin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> By the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Indie_pop" title="Indie pop">indie pop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> bands such as <a href="/wiki/The_Alarm" title="The Alarm">The Alarm</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Pooh_Sticks" title="The Pooh Sticks">The Pooh Sticks</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Darling_Buds" title="The Darling Buds">The Darling Buds</a> were popular in their genres. But the wider view at the time was that the wider Welsh music scene was stagnant, as the more popular musicians from Wales were from earlier eras.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwens20003-4_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwens20003-4-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990s, in England, the <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> scene was emerging, while in Wales, bands such as <a href="/wiki/Y_Cyrff" title="Y Cyrff">Y Cyrff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ffa_Coffi_Pawb" title="Ffa Coffi Pawb">Ffa Coffi Pawb</a> began to sing in English, starting an evolution<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that would lead to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Catatonia_(band)" title="Catatonia (band)">Catatonia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Super_Furry_Animals" title="Super Furry Animals">Super Furry Animals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cool_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cool-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The influence of the 80s bands and the emergence of a Welsh language and dual language music scene locally in Wales led to a dramatic shift in opinion across the United Kingdom as the "Cool Cymru" bands of the period emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwens20003-4_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwens20003-4-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The leading Welsh band during this period was the <a href="/wiki/Manic_Street_Preachers" title="Manic Street Preachers">Manic Street Preachers</a>, whose 1996 album <i><a href="/wiki/Everything_Must_Go_(Manic_Street_Preachers_album)" title="Everything Must Go (Manic Street Preachers album)">Everything Must Go</a></i> has been listed among the greatest albums of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of those bands have had ongoing success, while the general popularity of Welsh music during this period led to a resurgence of singers such as Tom Jones with his album <i><a href="/wiki/Reload_(Tom_Jones_album)" title="Reload (Tom Jones album)">Reload</a></i>. It was his first non-compilation number one album since 1968's <i><a href="/wiki/Delilah_(Tom_Jones_album)" title="Delilah (Tom Jones album)">Delilah</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Shirley Bassey reached the top 20 once more in the UK Charts with her collaboration with the <a href="/wiki/Propellerheads" title="Propellerheads">Propellerheads</a> on the single "<a href="/wiki/History_Repeating_(song)" title="History Repeating (song)">History Repeating</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> They also introduced new acts, such as Catatonia's <a href="/wiki/Owen_Powell" title="Owen Powell">Owen Powell</a> working with <a href="/wiki/Duffy_(singer)" title="Duffy (singer)">Duffy</a> during her early period.<sup id="cite_ref-cool_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cool-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Moving into the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Bullet_For_My_Valentine" class="mw-redirect" title="Bullet For My Valentine">Bullet For My Valentine</a> were named the Best British Band at the <a href="/wiki/Kerrang!_Awards" title="Kerrang! Awards">Kerrang! Awards</a> for three years running.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Other successful bands from this period include <a href="/wiki/Funeral_For_A_Friend" class="mw-redirect" title="Funeral For A Friend">Funeral For A Friend</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-rocksound_151_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rocksound_151-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lostprophets" title="Lostprophets">Lostprophets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Wales" title="Media of Wales">Media of Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Wales" title="List of newspapers in Wales">List of newspapers in Wales</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Wales" title="Sport in Wales">Sport in Wales</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg/170px-Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg" width="170" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg/255px-Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg 2x" data-file-width="333" data-file-height="490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michael_Owen_takes_a_lineout.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rugby Union action from Wales vs. England in 2006</div></div></div>
<p>Over fifty <a href="/wiki/Governing_bodies_of_sports_in_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Governing bodies of sports in Wales">national governing bodies</a> regulate and organise their sports in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Most of those involved in competitive sports select, organise and manage individuals or teams to represent their country at international events or fixtures against other countries. Wales is represented at major world sporting events such as the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>, Welsh athletes compete alongside those of Scotland, England and Northern Ireland as part of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_at_the_Olympics" title="Great Britain at the Olympics">Great Britain</a> team.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">Rugby union</a> is seen as a symbol of Welsh identity and an expression of national consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008782_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesJenkins2008782-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wales_national_rugby_union_team" title="Wales national rugby union team">The Welsh national rugby union team</a> takes part in the annual <a href="/wiki/Six_Nations_Championship" title="Six Nations Championship">Six Nations Championship</a> and has also competed in every <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> with Wales hosting the <a href="/wiki/1999_Rugby_World_Cup" title="1999 Rugby World Cup">1999 tournament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The five professional sides that replaced the traditional club sides in major competitions in 2003 were in turn replaced in 2004 by the four regions: <a href="/wiki/Scarlets" title="Scarlets">Scarlets</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Blues" title="Cardiff Blues">Cardiff Blues</a>; <a href="/wiki/Dragons_(rugby_union)" title="Dragons (rugby union)">Newport Gwent Dragons</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Ospreys_(rugby_union)" title="Ospreys (rugby union)">Ospreys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The Welsh regional teams play in the <a href="/wiki/Pro14" title="Pro14">Pro14</a> league,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Welsh_Cup" title="Anglo-Welsh Cup">Anglo-Welsh Cup</a> (LV Cup),<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Heineken_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="Heineken Cup">European Heineken Cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Challenge_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Challenge Cup">European (Amlin) Challenge Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wales has had its own <a href="/wiki/League_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="League of Wales">association football league</a> since 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> For historical and other reasons, two Welsh clubs (<a href="/wiki/Cardiff_City_F.C." title="Cardiff City F.C.">Cardiff City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swansea_City_A.F.C." title="Swansea City A.F.C.">Swansea City</a>) play in the <a href="/wiki/The_Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="The Football League">English Football League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Another four Welsh clubs play in English football's feeder leagues: <a href="/wiki/Wrexham_A.F.C." title="Wrexham A.F.C.">Wrexham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newport_County_A.F.C" class="mw-redirect" title="Newport County A.F.C">Newport County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merthyr_Town_F.C." title="Merthyr Town F.C.">Merthyr Town</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colwyn_Bay_F.C." title="Colwyn Bay F.C.">Colwyn Bay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uefawales_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uefawales-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> This also qualifies those teams to compete for England's domestic trophies. On 23 April 1927, Cardiff City became the only team outside England to win the <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In European football competitions, only teams playing in the Welsh leagues are eligible to play for Wales. The six teams in the English leagues are eligible to represent England only, and they are not allowed to compete for domestic Welsh trophies.<sup id="cite_ref-uefawales_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uefawales-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg/220px-Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg/330px-Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg/440px-Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="755" data-file-height="853" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tanni_Grey-Thompson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welsh athlete Tanni Grey-Thompson won 11 Paralympic gold medals</div></div></div>
<p>In international <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, Wales and England field a single representative team, administered by the <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales_Cricket_Board" title="England and Wales Cricket Board">England and Wales Cricket Board</a> (ECB), called the <a href="/wiki/England_cricket_team" title="England cricket team">England cricket team</a>, or simply "England".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, a separate <a href="/wiki/Wales_national_cricket_team" title="Wales national cricket team">Wales national cricket team</a> plays in limited-overs competitions, mainly against English county teams.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan_County_Cricket_Club" title="Glamorgan County Cricket Club">Glamorgan</a> is the only Welsh participant in the England and Wales County Championship.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a> have argued that <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> should have its own international team and withdraw from the existing arrangement under which Welsh players play for England. The proposal has aroused opposition from Cricket Wales and Glamorgan County Cricket Club, who argue such a move would be financially disastrous. The debate focused on a report produced by the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">Welsh National Assembly’s</a> petitions committee, which reflected the arguments on both sides. <a href="/wiki/Bethan_Jenkins" class="mw-redirect" title="Bethan Jenkins">Bethan Jenkins</a>, Plaid Cymru’s spokesperson on heritage, culture, sport and broadcasting, and a member of the petitions committee, said: "Cricket Wales and Glamorgan CCC say the idea of a Welsh national cricket team is ‘an emotive subject’. Of course having a national team is emotive. You only have to look at the stands during any national game to see that. To suggest this as anything other than natural is a bit of a misleading argument."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
In their strategic plan, Cricket Wales state they are "committed to continuing to play a major role within the ECB"<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Wales has produced several world-class participants in individual sports, including <a href="/wiki/Snooker" title="Snooker">snooker</a> players <a href="/wiki/Ray_Reardon" title="Ray Reardon">Ray Reardon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terry_Griffiths" title="Terry Griffiths">Terry Griffiths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Williams_(snooker_player)" title="Mark Williams (snooker player)">Mark Williams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Stevens" title="Matthew Stevens">Matthew Stevens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Successful track athletes include miler <a href="/wiki/Jim_Alford" title="Jim Alford">Jim Alford</a> who was a world record holder in the 4 x 1500 metres relay, the 110-metre hurdler <a href="/wiki/Colin_Jackson" title="Colin Jackson">Colin Jackson</a> who is a former world record holder and the winner of numerous Olympic, World and European medals,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tanni_Grey-Thompson" title="Tanni Grey-Thompson">Tanni Grey-Thompson</a> who has won 11 Paralympic gold medals.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Wales has also produced a number of world-class boxers. <a href="/wiki/Joe_Calzaghe" title="Joe Calzaghe">Joe Calzaghe</a> was <a href="/wiki/WBO" class="mw-redirect" title="WBO">WBO</a> World Super-Middleweight Champion and then won the WBA, WBC and Ring Magazine super-middleweight and Ring Magazine Light-Heavyweight titles.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Other former boxing world champions include <a href="/wiki/Enzo_Maccarinelli" title="Enzo Maccarinelli">Enzo Maccarinelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Welsh" title="Freddie Welsh">Freddie Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Winstone" title="Howard Winstone">Howard Winstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Jones_(boxer)" title="Percy Jones (boxer)">Percy Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Wilde" title="Jimmy Wilde">Jimmy Wilde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Robinson_(boxer)" title="Steve Robinson (boxer)">Steve Robinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robbie_Regan" title="Robbie Regan">Robbie Regan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_cuisine" title="Welsh cuisine">Welsh cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg/220px-White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg/330px-White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg/440px-White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Welsh_Mountain_sheep.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A white <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Mountain_Sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Mountain Sheep">Welsh Mountain Sheep</a></div></div></div>
<p>Wales is not considered to have a strong food identity, with some people considering that there is "no such thing as Welsh food".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19968_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19968-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Welsh cookery is said to be similar to <a href="/wiki/English_Cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="English Cuisine">English Cuisine</a> in style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman199614_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman199614-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> However, there are regional variations in the food seen across Wales, which can be traced historically to the availability of certain crops and produce in specific areas of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman199618_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman199618-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Gower" title="Cuisine of Gower">cuisine of Gower</a> is particularly different to the rest of Wales. It was strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, and developed dishes such as <a href="/wiki/Whitepot" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitepot">whitepot</a> while ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin" title="Pumpkin">pumpkin</a> were used, which are unusual in the rest of Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman199622_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman199622-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle farming produces the majority of Wales' agricultural output. Welsh beef is protected under <a href="/wiki/Geographical_indications_and_traditional_specialities_in_the_European_Union" title="Geographical indications and traditional specialities in the European Union">European Union law</a>, meaning that it must be produced and slaughtered in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Welsh_pig" title="Welsh pig">Welsh pigs</a> are raised, providing good cuts of meat.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The mountainous areas of Wales are suited to <a href="/wiki/Sheep_farming_in_Wales" title="Sheep farming in Wales">sheep farming</a> and this has led to an association of their meat with the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheep_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheep-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mutton" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutton">mutton</a> of Wales has been popular in the rest of the United Kingdom since the 16th century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman199837-38_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman199837-38-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and by the end of the 20th century there were more than 11 million sheep in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheep_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheep-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cawl_Cymreig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cawl_Cymreig.jpg/220px-Cawl_Cymreig.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cawl_Cymreig.jpg/330px-Cawl_Cymreig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cawl_Cymreig.jpg/440px-Cawl_Cymreig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="459" data-file-height="315" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cawl_Cymreig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cawl, a Welsh dish of meat and vegetables</div></div></div>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/List_of_Welsh_dishes" title="List of Welsh dishes">Welsh dishes</a> are thought of because their ingredients are associated with Wales, whereas others have been developed there. <a href="/wiki/Cawl" title="Cawl">Cawl</a> is regarded as the Welsh national dish;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb201268_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb201268-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> it is a slow-cooked meat and vegetable broth. Traditionally it was a vegetable-heavy dish,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman199620_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman199620-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> but now it is more likely to contain beef or lamb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson2014154_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson2014154-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Welsh_rarebit" title="Welsh rarebit">Welsh rarebit</a> is thought to date from the 18th century, although the original term "Welsh rabbit" may have been intended as a slur against the Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Another use of cheese in a traditional Welsh dish is seen in <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan_sausage" title="Glamorgan sausage">Glamorgan sausage</a>, which is a skinless sausage made of cheese and either leek or spring onion,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> which is then rolled into a sausage shape before frying.<sup id="cite_ref-Symbols_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbols-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a
